Question 1 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A peptic ulcer in a sore that forms where the lining of the stomach or duodenum (the first part of the small intestine) has been eaten away by stomach acids or digestive juices. In addition to Helicobacter pylori infection of the stomach, what is the most common cause of peptic ulcer?

Options:
  Consuming very spicy foods
  Cigarette smoking
  Moderate use of alcohol
✓ Use of nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs)
  None of the above

Explanation:
The two major causes of peptic ulcer disease are H. pylori infection and the use of NSAIDs.

Question 2 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Typhoid disease usually involves which of the following:

Options:
  Jejunum
  Duodenum
  Rectum
✓ Ileum
  Colon

Explanation:
Salmonella Typhi predominantly affects the ileum, specifically the Peyer's patches (lymphoid tissue).

Question 3 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Risk factors for the development of colorectal cancer involves:

Options:
  Colonic polyps
  IBD
  Age >50 years
  Low fibre diet
✓ All of the above

Explanation:
All listed factors (adenomatous polyps, inflammatory bowel disease, advancing age, and low-fiber/high-fat diet) are established risk factors for colorectal cancer.

Question 4 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Most common tumour of biliary system is:

Options:
✓ CA gall bladder
  Cholangiocarcinoma
  Ampullary carcinoma
  CA head of pancreas
  Hepatocellular carcinoma

Explanation:
Carcinoma of the gallbladder is the most common malignancy of the biliary tract.

Question 5 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Benign causes of gastric outlet obstruction involved all except:

Options:
  Peptic ulcer disease
  Gastric polyps
  Pyloric stenosis
  Pancreatic pseudocysts
✓ Worm infestation

Explanation:
While heavy worm infestation (Ascaris) can cause intestinal obstruction, it is not a classic or common cause of gastric outlet obstruction compared to the other listed benign conditions (ulcer stricture, polyps, pyloric stenosis, extrinsic compression by pseudocyst).

Question 6 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Most common symptom for Gall stone is:

Options:
✓ Pain RHC (Right Hypochondrium)
  Vomiting
  Constipation
  Jaundice
  Fever

Explanation:
Biliary colic, manifesting as pain in the right hypochondrium (RHC) or epigastrium, is the most common symptomatic presentation of gallstones.

Question 7 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Majority of Gall Stones are:

Options:
✓ Cholesterol stones
  Pigment stones
  Brown pigment stones
  Mixed stones
  Both a and c

Explanation:
Cholesterol stones account for the majority (>80%) of gallstones in Western populations.

Question 8 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Jaundice in viral Hepatitis can be classified under:

Options:
  Congenital hyperbilirubinemia
  Haemolytic
✓ Hepatocellular
  Obstructive
  None of the above

Explanation:
Viral hepatitis causes damage to hepatocytes, leading to hepatocellular jaundice (mixed conjugated and unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia).

================================================================================
Question 9 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Pathogenesis of cholesterol gallstones include except:

Options:
  Cholesterol deposition
  Bile stasis
  Low bilirubin concentration
✓ High bilirubin concentration
  Dehydration

Explanation:
High bilirubin concentration predisposes to pigment stones, not cholesterol stones. Cholesterol stones form due to bile supersaturation with cholesterol, hypomotility (stasis), and nucleation.

================================================================================
Question 10 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Hepatitis A in children can progress to;

Options:
  Liver cirrhosis
✓ Persistent cholestatic jaundice
  Hepatorenal syndrome
  Megaloblastic anemia
  Viral myocarditis

Explanation:
Hepatitis A does not cause chronic liver disease or cirrhosis. However, a variant known as cholestatic hepatitis A can cause prolonged/persistent jaundice.

Question 11 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old female with persistent GERD underwent endoscopy. On OGD there was suspicion of Barrett's esophagus so a biopsy was taken. Biopsy showed low grade dysplasia on histopathology. The patient is at greatest risk for which of the following:

Options:
  Squamous cell carcinoma
✓ Adenocarcinoma
  Peptic stricture
  Pernicious anemia
  None of the above

Explanation:
Barrett's esophagus with dysplasia is a direct precursor to Esophageal Adenocarcinoma.

Question 12 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following type of gastritis is associated with pernicious anemia?

Options:
  Diffuse antral Gastritis
  Environmental metaplastic atrophic gastritis (EMAG)
  Collagenous colitis
✓ Autoimmune metaplastic atrophic gastritis (AMAG)
  None of the above

Explanation:
Autoimmune Metaplastic Atrophic Gastritis (AMAG) destroys parietal cells, leading to intrinsic factor deficiency and Pernicious Anemia.

Question 13 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
Which one of the following conditions commonly predisposes to colonic carcinoma?

Options:
✓ Ulcerative colitis
  Crohn's disease
  Diverticuler disease
  Ischemic colitis
  IBS

Explanation:
Ulcerative Colitis is a significant risk factor for colorectal carcinoma, with the risk increasing with the duration and extent of the disease.

Question 14 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following is most commonly associated with adenocarcinoma of the oesophagus?

Options:
  Achalasia cardia
✓ Barrett's oesophagus
  Chronic smoking
  Plummer vinson syndrome
  Lynch Syndrome

Explanation:
Barrett's esophagus is the most important risk factor for esophageal adenocarcinoma. (Smoking and Plummer Vinson are more associated with Squamous Cell Carcinoma).

Question 15 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following would be the best morphological feature to distinguish ulcerative colitis from Crohn's disease?

Options:
  Diffuse distributions of pseudopolyps
  Mucosal edema
  Crypt abscesses
  Lymphoid aggregates in the mucosa
✓ None of the above

Explanation:
The distinguishing features are typically transmural inflammation, skip lesions, and granulomas (Crohn's) versus mucosal inflammation and continuous involvement (UC). None of the listed options (pseudopolyps, edema, crypt abscesses, lymphoid aggregates) are definitive distinctive features as they can occur in both, although frequencies differ.

================================================================================
Question 16 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
In which of the following conditions of malabsorption, an intestinal biopsy is diagnostic?

Options:
  Tropical sprue
  Celiac disease
✓ Whipple's disease
  Lactose intolerance
  IBS

Explanation:
Whipple's disease has pathognomonic histological findings (PAS-positive foamy macrophages in the lamina propria), making biopsy diagnostic.

Question 17 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Upper GI endoscopy and biopsy from lower esophagus in a 48-year-old lady with chronic heart burn shows presence of columnar epithelium with goblet cell. The feature is most likely consistent with?

Options:
  Dysplasia
  Hyperplasia
  Carcinoma in-situ
✓ Metaplasia
  Anaplasia

Explanation:
The replacement of squamous epithelium by columnar epithelium with goblet cells is the definition of Intestinal Metaplasia (Barrett's Esophagus).

Question 18 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
Most common mechanism of GERD?

Options:
  Gastritis
  Hiatus Hernia
  Hypotension at lower esophageal sphincter
✓ Transient decrease in pressure at LES
  Varices

Explanation:
The most common mechanism for Gastroesophageal Reflux Disease (GERD) is transient relaxation of the Lower Esophageal Sphincter (tLESr).

Question 19 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with cirrhosis is at risk for developing complications. Which condition is most serious and potentially life threatening?

Options:
  Ascites
✓ Oesophageal varices
  Gastric ulcer
  Hepatomegaly
  Peripheral edema

Explanation:
Rupture of esophageal varices causes massive upper gastrointestinal hemorrhage and is a leading cause of death in cirrhotic patients.

Question 20 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Bilirubin which is insoluble in water is called?

Options:
  Conjugated bilirubin
  Direct bilirubin
  Indirect bilirubin
✓ Unconjugated bilirubin
  Urobilinogen

Explanation:
Unconjugated bilirubin (also called Indirect bilirubin) is water-insoluble and requires albumin for transport.

  Cecum

  Pancreas

Question 22 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 65 year old male patient was diagnosed with Gastric carcinoma. His biopsy report findings are diffuse infiltrative type (Linitis plastica) gastric cancer typically composed of signet ring cells. The gastric wall is thickened with partial loss of rugal folds but no prominent mass is present. Which of the following mutation is key step in development of this type of Gastric carcinoma?

Options:
  APC mutations
✓ E-cadherin loss
  CDKN2A mutation
  TGF-beta mutation
  Wnt pathway

Explanation:
Loss of function mutations in the CDH1 gene, which encodes E-cadherin, is the key molecular alteration in Diffuse Gastric Cancer.

Question 23 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year old lady was diagnosed with gastric carcinoma having metastasis in ovaries at the time of diagnosis. The biopsy report showed numerous signet ring cells. What is this metastatic gastric carcinoma in ovaries called?

Options:
✓ Krukenberg tumor
  Lobular carcinoma
  Pouch of douglas tumor
  Sister Mary Joseph nodule
  Virchow node

Explanation:
A Krukenberg tumor refers to malignancy in the ovary that metastasized from a primary site, classically the stomach (signet ring cell carcinoma).

Question 24 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following is the most powerful prognostic indicator of gastric cancer?

Options:
  Barret's esophagus
  CDH1 mutation
✓ Depth of invasion
  H. pylori gastritis
  Peptic ulcer disease

Explanation:
The depth of invasion (and nodal status) determines the Tumor Stage, which is the most powerful prognostic indicator.

Question 26 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
High risk transmission for HCV:

Options:
  Breast feeding
  Fomite transmission
✓ Needle stick injury
  Sexual transmission
  Vertical transmission

Explanation:
Hepatitis C is primarily transmitted parenterally; needle stick injuries and IV drug use are high-risk modes. Sexual and vertical transmission are less common compared to Hep B.

Options:
  Alcoholic liver disease
  Crohn disease
  Hepatitis B
  Irritable bowel syndrome
✓ Ulcerative colitis

Question 28 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Granulomas are present in which of the following?

Options:
✓ Crohn disease
  Hepatitis A
  Hepatitis C
  Liver cirrhosis
  Ulcerative colitis

Explanation:
Non-caseating granulomas are a hallmark histological feature of Crohn's disease, distinguishing it from Ulcerative Colitis.

Question 29 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Crohn disease is also known as:

Options:
  Granulomatous disease
✓ Terminal ileitis
  Irritable bowel syndrome
  Intestinal perforation
  Toxic megacolon

Explanation:
Crohn's disease most commonly affects the terminal ileum, leading to the older name 'Terminal Ileitis' or 'Regional Enteritis'.

Question 30 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following part of the intestine is involved in Ulcerative colitis?

Options:
✓ Colon and rectum
  Duodenum
  From mouth to anus
  Ileum
  Small intestine

Explanation:
Ulcerative colitis involves the rectum and extends proximally into the colon, typically sparing the small intestine (except for backwash ileitis).

================================================================================

Question:
Skin lesions are present in which of the following?

Options:
  CA colon
  Cirrhosis of liver
✓ Crohn disease
  Hepatitis A
  Ulcerative colitis

Explanation:
Extra-intestinal skin manifestations like Erythema Nodosum are commonly associated with Inflammatory Bowel Disease, particularly Crohn's Disease.

Question 32 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Choose the incorrect: Hepatitis D virus (HDV):

Options:
  Is directly causing acute hepatitis
  Co-infection with hepatitis B virus (HBV)
  Super infection in HBs antigen positive patient
  Combined with HBV is a cause of fulminant hepatitis
✓ No vaccine can prevent HDV infection

Explanation:
This statement is incorrect because vaccination against Hepatitis B prevents Hepatitis D infection, as HDV requires HBV for its replication.

Question:
Hepatotropic viruses are:

Options:
  A, B and C
  A, B, C and D
✓ A, B, C, and E
  None of the above
  A, B, C, E, and cytomegalovirus

Explanation:
The primary hepatotropic viruses are Hepatitis A, B, C, D, and E. Option (c) is the best available subset among the choices, though it omits D.

Question 34 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Microscopic examination of a histopathology slide shows Rokitansky Aschoff sinuses, the site of biopsy following?

Options:
  Liver
  Stomach
✓ Gall bladder
  Small intestine
  Rectum

Explanation:
Rokitansky-Aschoff sinuses are mucosal outpouchings into the muscular layer characteristic of the Gall bladder (chronic cholecystitis).

Options:
✓ Helicobacter pylori infection
  NSAIDS
  Zollinger ellisson syndrome
  Cigarette smoking
  Eosinophilic gastritis

Question 36 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 68-year-old patient with cardiac failure is diagnosed with ovarian cancer. She is started on cisplatin but becomes nauseous and suffers from severe vomiting. Which of the following medications would be most effective to counteract the emesis in this patient without exacerbating her cardiac problem?

Options:
  Droperidol
  Dolasetron
  Dronabinol
✓ Ondansetron
  Prochlorperazine

Explanation:
Ondansetron is effective for cisplatin-induced emesis and has a better safety profile for cardiac patients compared to Dolasetron (QT prolongation risk) or Droperidol.

Question 37 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45-year-old woman is distressed by the dissolution of her marriage. She has been drinking heavily and overeating. She complains of persistent heartburn and an unpleasant, acid like taste in her mouth. The clinician suspects gastrointestinal reflux disease and advises her to raise the head of her bed 6 to 8 inches, not to eat for several hours before retiring, to avoid alcohol, and to eat smaller meals. Two weeks later, she returns and says the symptoms have subsided slightly but still are a concern. The clinician prescribes:

Options:
  An antacid such as Aluminum hydroxide
  An antianxiety agent such as alprazolam
  Dicyclomine
✓ Esomeprazole
  Tetracycline

Explanation:
For persistent GERD symptoms despite lifestyle modifications, a Proton Pump Inhibitor (PPI) like Esomeprazole is the most effective pharmacologic treatment.

Question 38 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following agents interferes with most of the cytochrome P450 enzymes and, thus, leads to many drug-drug interactions?

Options:
✓ Cimetidine
  Famotidine
  Ondansetron
  Omeprazole
  Sucralfate

Explanation:
Cimetidine is a broad inhibitor of CYP450 enzymes, causing interactions with drugs like warfarin, phenytoin, and theophylline.

Question 39 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A couple celebrating their fortieth wedding anniversary is given a trip to Peru to visit Machu Picchu. Due to past experiences while traveling, they ask their doctor to prescribe an agent for diarrhea. Which of the following would be effective?

Options:
  Amoxycillin
  Famotidine
✓ Loperamide
  Lorazepam
  Omeprazol

Explanation:
Loperamide is an opioid agonist that slows gut motility and is commonly used for the symptomatic treatment of traveler's diarrhea.

Question:
A 56-year-old man from South America is found to be parasitized by both schistosomes and Taenia solium the pork tapeworm. Which of the following anthelmintic drugs would be effective for both infestations?

Options:
  Albendazole
  Ivermectin
  Mebendazole
  Niclosamide
✓ Praziquantel

Praziquantel is the drug of choice for Schistosomiasis and is also effective against Taenia solium (tapeworms).

Question:
Identify the mucosal protective agent from the given anti-peptic ulcer drugs?

Options:
  Sodium bicarbonate
  Aluminum hydroxide
  Famotidine
✓ Bismuth subcitrate
  All of the above

Explanation:
Bismuth compounds (like Bismuth subcitrate) coat ulcers and erosions, creating a protective layer against acid and pepsin.

Question 45 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Extrapyramidal symptoms (EPS) have been associated with which of the following drug?

Options:
✓ Metoclopramide
  Alprazolam
  Aprepitant
  Loperamide
  Misoprostol

Explanation:
Metoclopramide blocks dopamine D2 receptors in the CNS, leading to extrapyramidal symptoms like dystonia and parkinsonism.

Question:
Which one of the following best describes the mechanism of action of ribavirin?

Options:
  Inhibits viral aspartate protease
  Inhibits viral DNA polymerase
✓ Inhibits viral RNA polymerase
  Inhibits viral reverse transcriptase
  Prevent integration of viral genome

Explanation:
Ribavirin inhibits viral RNA polymerase (and other mechanisms like GTP depletion) to inhibit RNA virus replication.

Question 48 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The following helminthic disease can be treated by albendazole but not by mebendazole:

Options:
  Hookworm infestation
  Threadworm infestation
  Trichuriasis
✓ Neurocysticercosis
  Nematodes

Explanation:
Albendazole has better systemic absorption and penetration into the CNS compared to Mebendazole, making it effective for Neurocysticercosis.

Question 49 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The primary role of sulfasalazine in ulcerative colitis is:

Options:
  Suppression of enteroinvasive pathogens
  Control of acute exacerbations of the disease
  Maintenance of remission
✓ Both b and c
  None of the above

Explanation:
Sulfasalazine is used both for inducing remission in mild-to-moderate ulcerative colitis and for maintaining remission.

Question 50 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 39 year old carpenter has taken 2 bottles of liquor from the local shop After about an hour he develops confusion, vomiting and blurring of vision. He has been brought to the emergency department. He should be given:

Options:
  Naloxone
  Diazepam
  Flumazenil
✓ Ethyl Alcohol
  Atropine

Explanation:
The symptoms (blurring of vision, confusion) suggest Methanol poisoning. Ethanol acts as a competitive inhibitor for Alcohol Dehydrogenase, preventing the formation of toxic metabolites (Fomepizole is preferred if available, but Ethanol is a standard answer).

================================================================================
Question 51 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Scoring system sued for diagnosis of acute appendicitis

Options:
  Glasgow score
  Ranson score
✓ Alvarado score

Explanation:
The Alvarado score (MANTRELS score) is a clinical scoring system used to diagnose acute appendicitis. Glasgow and Ranson scores are used for Pancreatitis.

Question 52 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 year old male, known hypertensive, history of Ischaemic disease, recently episode of Atrial fibrillation presented with sudden onset of abdominal pain in last 6 hours, generalized and continuous pain BP 100/70 Pulse 150/min, irregularly most likely clinical diagnosis

Options:
  Angiodysplasia
✓ Mesenteric Ischaemia
  Inflammatory Bowel disease
  Myocardial infarction
  Pancreatitis

Explanation:
Sudden severe abdominal pain in a patient with Atrial Fibrillation (a source for thromboembolism) is highly suggestive of Acute Mesenteric Ischemia.

Question 53 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 3-years-old male child presented in outpatient department with history of bulky, greasy and foul smelling stool for the last one year. On examination, patient is under weight, pale with clubbing of digits and having distended abdomen. The most likely diagnosis,

Options:
  Lactose intolerance
✓ Celiac disease
  Inflammatory bowel disease
  Acrodermatitis enteropathica
  Giardiasis

Explanation:
The clinical triad of malabsorption (steatorrhea, weight loss), abdominal distension, and pallor in a toddler is classic for Celiac Disease.

Question 54 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
A 9-months-old infant was rehydrated in emergency department with Normal saline 30ml/kg in one hour and 70ml/kg in next 5 hours. The most likely clinical presentation was;

Options:
  Restless and irritable
✓ Lethargic
  Slow skin pinch
  Seizure

Explanation:
Rapid IV rehydration (Plan C: 100ml/kg total) is indicated for Severe Dehydration, signs of which include lethargy or unconsciousness.

Question 55 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
What is the predominant route of transmission for HEV infection?

Options:
  Person-to-person contact
✓ Consumption of contaminated drinking water
  Vertical transmission from mother to child
  Parenteral route of infection
  Transmission by blood transfusion

Explanation:
Hepatitis E Virus (HEV) is primarily transmitted via the fecal-oral route, most commonly through contaminated drinking water.

Question 56 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 year old man comes to your office for a follow op unit. One year ago, he was diagnosed with hereditary hemochromatosis after routine blood work found elevated ferritin and genetic testing revealed he had a C282Y/H63D genotype. He has been treated by phlebotomy with one unit of blood removed every week for the past year. His most recent laboratory results include the following Hemoglobin 120 g/l, Hematocrit 36%, Serum ferritin 50 ng/ml, Serum iron 100 ug/dl, Serum total IBC 260 mg/dl. What is the most appropriate next step in his Therapy?

Options:
✓ Phlebotomy 1 unt every 3 months
  A low Iron diet
  Phlebotomy 1 unit every 2 weeks until the hemoglobin concentration is less than 10.0g/dL
  Liver biopsy to assess hepatic overload
  Phlebotomy 1 unit every 2 weeks until the serum iron level is less than 20 ug/Dl

Explanation:
The goal of maintenance therapy in hemochromatosis is to keep serum ferritin between 50-100 ng/mL. Since the patient has reached the target (50 ng/mL), the frequency of phlebotomy can be reduced to maintenance levels (e.g., every 3-4 months).

================================================================================
Question 57 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6 year old Asian man with chronic HAV and cirrhous referred to your clinic He is not currently on HBV treatment. A recent abdominal ultrasound did not show any suspicious lesion in the liver its laboratory values are an Follows AST 45 U/L, ALT 34 U/L, HBeAg negative, HBeAb positive, HBV DNA 3000 / ml3. Total bilirubin 1 mg/dL What is the most appropriate recommendation at this time

Options:
  A liver biopsy to assess for inflammation
  An abdominal MRI
✓ Initiating antiviral treatment with tenofovir
  Holding antiviral treatment for now and monitor Liver function tests every 6 months
  Treatment with interferon

Explanation:
In patients with compensated cirrhosis and detectable HBV DNA (even low levels >2000 IU/mL), antiviral treatment is recommended to prevent decompensation and HCC. Tenofovir is a first-line agent.

Question 58 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old patient presented with history of progressive dysphagia which is both for solids and liquids There is a history of foul-smelling vomiting A manometric study was advised based on barium swallow. The manometry showed absent peristalsis and increased lower esophageal sphincter pressure. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Diffuse esophageal spasm
  Esophageal carcinoma
✓ Achalasia cardia
  Scleroderma
  None of the above

Explanation:
The manometric findings of aperistalsis and incomplete LES relaxation (or high pressure) are diagnostic for Achalasia.

Question 59 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 year-old patient with known ulcerative colitis presented with acute flare of the disease due to non compliance of therapy which was severe in intensity. He was put on tab mesalamine 800 mg TDS and i/v prednisolone at a dose of 1 mg/kg/day On 6 day there was no response to treatment as evaluated by Truelove and witts criteria. Patient is otherwise hemodynamically stable. What will you do next?

Options:
  Pan colectomy
✓ Start on I/v cyclosporin
  Double the dose of steroids
  Add azathioprine to above treatment
  None of the above

Explanation:
For acute severe ulcerative colitis refractory to IV corticosteroids (usually assessed at day 3-5), rescue therapy with Infliximab or Cyclosporine is indicated before considering colectomy.

Question 60 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the test is done to detect latent tuberculosis?

Options:
  Smear staining
  Nuclear acid amplification
  Culture
✓ Mantoux test
  All of the above

Explanation:
The Mantoux tuberculin skin test (TST) and Interferon-Gamma Release Assays (IGRAs) are used to detect Latent TB Infection. Smear and culture are for active disease.

Question 61 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old women was presented with chronic NSAID use for osteoarthritis of her knees is admitted to Hospital for hematemesis. On physical examination her vital signs are as follows: Temp 37.5, BP 90/50 Heart rate 130. Respiratory rate 17/min She is resuscitated and EGD was performed. A large 4 cm ulcer noted in antrum with oozing of blood. Which of the following is most appropriate next step in management?

Options:
  No endoscopic therapy, start PPI drip
  Send the patient to interventional radiology for further therapy
✓ Dual endoscopic therapy and PPI drip for 72 hrs
  Billroth 1 gastric surgery
  None of the above

Explanation:
Active bleeding (Forrest Ib) requires endoscopic hemostasis (usually dual therapy like epinephrine injection + thermal/mechanical method) followed by high-dose IV PPI.

================================================================================
Question 62 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old patent was admitted in hospital for pneumonia He was put on antibiotics and while recovering from Pneumonia He suddenly developed pain abdomen, watery diarrhea and fever. His labs are as follow Hb 10.5. TLC 25000 Creatine 1.9 ALT 56 CRP positive What is the most appropriate investigation to evaluate the cause of Diarrhea in this patient?

Options:
  Stool R/E
  Fecal calprotection level
✓ Clostridium difficile toxin B assay in stool
  Sigmoidscopy with biopsy
  None of the above

Explanation:
The clinical scenario of diarrhea following antibiotic use, accompanied by leukocytosis and fever, strongly suggests Clostridium difficile infection (CDI). Toxin assay is the diagnostic test.

================================================================================
Question 63 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 39 year old woman presents in OPD with one month history of nausea and vague pain epigastrium that is worst after meal She denses weight loss, vomiting, dysphagia, hematemesis and rectal bleeding. She is taking contraceptives and occasional ibuprofen for headaches. Physical examination revealed mild tenderness in epigastrium. She was advised to stop Ibuprofen and started on pantoprazole. She returnes to OPD after 8 weeks but her symptoms are not changed. What is ther best therapeutic test?

Options:
  Endoscopy with random biopsy
  H. pylori serology and treatment if positive
✓ H.pylori stool antigen testing and treatment if positive
  Empirically treat H. pylori
  None of the above

Explanation:
For persistent dyspepsia (uninvestigated) in a young patient without alarm features, a 'test and treat' strategy for H. pylori is appropriate. Stool antigen or Urea Breath Test are preferred over serology for active infection.

================================================================================
Question 64 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 year old male was admitted with high grade continuous fever, malaise, arthralgia and loose stool for 6 days. O/E his temp 100 F, pulse-78/min and has splenomegaly. CBC shows leucopenia. On the basis of above information we are suspecting enteric fever. What next investigation will you do to confirm your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Blood culture
  Stool culture
  Widal test
  Ultrasound abdomen
  Urine culture

Explanation:
In the first week of Enteric fever, blood culture is the most sensitive diagnostic investigation (BASU: Blood 1st week, Agglutination/Widal 2nd, Stool 3rd, Urine 4th).

================================================================================
Question 65 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
What is best used for detecting small or healing ulcers?

Options:
  Abdominal CT
  Barium radiography
✓ Endoscopy
  MRI
  Urea breathe best?

Explanation:
Endoscopy is the gold standard and most sensitive method for visualizing mucosal lesions, including small or healing ulcers.

================================================================================
Question 66 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient develops hepatic encephalopathy. Which clinical manifestation is most common with this condition?

Options:
✓ Altered level of consciousness
  Deceased tendon reflex
  Hypertension
  Hypotension
  Increased urine output

Explanation:
Hepatic encephalopathy is characterized by a spectrum of neuropsychiatric abnormalities, most notably an altered level of consciousness (confusion, lethargy, coma) and asterixis.

Question 67 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40-year-old male patient presented with jaundice, fever & abdominal pain. He also complained of vomiting and nausea. The provisional diagnosis of Liver abscess due to hydatid cysts is being considered Which of the following investigation will most likely help you in making a diagnosis?

Options:
  Blood complete picture
  Flow cytometry
  Immunohistochemistry
✓ Radiologic finding
  Stool culture

Explanation:
Imaging (Radiologic findings via Ultrasound or CT) is the primary diagnostic tool for liver abscesses/cysts. It reveals the characteristic appearance of hydatid cysts (daughter cysts, calcification).

Question 68 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
What is the most common symptom associated with chronic HCV infection?

Options:
  Abdominal pain with or without ascites
✓ Asymptomatic
  Dark stool
  Fever with or without jaundice
  Jaundice

Explanation:
The vast majority of patients with chronic Hepatitis C infection are asymptomatic for many years until advanced liver disease develops.

Question:
A 50-year-old male patient presents with abdominal pain, constipation, and rectal bleeding, He has a family history of colon cancer. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Colorectal adenoma
  Inflammatory bowel disease
✓ Colorectal carcinoma
  Hemorrhoids
  Diverticulitis

Explanation:
The combination of rectal bleeding, change in bowel habits (constipation), abdominal pain, age 50, and family history strongly points to Colorectal Carcinoma.

Question 70 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
A 70-year-old male patient presents with weight loss, fatigue, and diarrhea. Imaging studies reveal a large mass in the colon with metastases to the liver. What is the most appropriate treatment for this patient?

Options:
  Endoscopic mucosal resection
  Surgical resection of the tumor
✓ Palliative chemotherapy
  Radiation therapy
  Observation only

Explanation:
For metastatic colon cancer (Stage IV) with distant metastases (liver), the primary treatment intent is usually palliative, involving systemic chemotherapy.

Question:
Which of the following symptoms is commonly associated with bacterial enterocolitis?

Options:
✓ Fever
  Joint pain
  Headache
  Dry cough
  Blurred vision

Explanation:
Bacterial enterocolitis (e.g., Salmonella, Shigella, Campylobacter) is typically an inflammatory process presenting with fever, abdominal pain, and diarrhea.

Question 72 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
A 14 years male came to OPD with symptoms and signs of malabsorption. He is suspected celiac disease. To confirm it which of the following is most appropriate?

Options:
  Full blood count serum ferritin/and vit-D level
  Tissue transglutaminase antibodies
  Family history of celiac disease
✓ Tissue transglutaminase antibodies followes by small intestinal endoscopic biopsy histopathology examination
  Antigliadin antibodies

Explanation:
The definitive diagnosis of Celiac disease requires positive serology (tTG) followed by confirmation with a duodenal biopsy showing characteristic histological changes.

Options:
  Liver cirrhosis
  Cholangio carcinoma
✓ Primary Sclerosing cholangitis
  NASH
  Hepatocellular carcinoma

"Onion skin" periductal fibrosis is the pathognomonic histological finding in Primary Sclerosing Cholangitis (PSC).

Question 74 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Diagnostic test for Wilson's disease is

Options:
  Antimitrochondrial antibodies
  Glucose intolerance
  Liver biopsy
  Serum copper level
✓ Serum ceruloplasmin

Explanation:
Low serum ceruloplasmin is the primary screening and diagnostic marker for Wilson's disease (along with urinary copper and Kayser-Fleischer rings).

Question 75 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 year, old female undergoes Appendectomy for acute appendicitis. Histopathology shows 1 cm carcinoid tumour at the tip of the appendix most appropriate further management is:

Options:
  Right hemicolectomy
  Right hemicolectomy with chemotherapy
  Chemotherapy alone
✓ No further treatment
  Radiotherapy

Explanation:
Carcinoid tumors of the appendix smaller than 2 cm and confined to the tip/distal appendix, and without mesoappendiceal invasion, are adequately treated by appendectomy alone. No further treatment is usually required.

Question 76 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following diagnostic tests is commonly used to detect bacterial enterocolitis?

Options:
  Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI)
  Computed tomography (CT) scan
  Blood culture
✓ Stool culture
  Urine analysis

Explanation:
Stool culture is the standard microbiological test used to identify bacterial pathogens causing enterocolitis.

================================================================================
Question 77 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Rigor Mortis last longer in poisoning due to:

Options:
✓ Arsenic
  Antimony
  Mercury
  Thallium
  Lead

Explanation:
Rigor mortis lasts longer in deaths due to certain inorganic poisons like arsenic and antimony because they inhibit postmortem bacterial putrefaction and speed up dehydration.

================================================================================
Question 78 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Gastric lavage is contraindicated in corrosive poisoning as it causes:

Options:
  Aspiration
  Embolism
✓ Perforation
  Stroke
  Stricture formation

Explanation:
Gastric lavage is contraindicated in corrosive poisoning due to the high risk of esophageal or gastric perforation.

================================================================================
Question 79 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The predominant symptoms in acute arsenic poisoning is:

Options:
  Changes in voice
  Cerebral irritation
  Hypothermia
  Hyperthermia
✓ Rice water stools

Explanation:
Acute arsenic poisoning causes severe, rapid gastroenteritis, resulting in profuse, watery stools resembling 'rice water stools' (similar to cholera).

Question 80 (GMC - 2023)

Raindrop appearance of the skin is seen in chronic poisoning due to:

Options:
  Barium carbonate
  Super warfarins
  Zinc phosphide
  Lead
✓ Arsenic

Explanation:
Raindrop pigmentation is a classic dermatological feature of chronic Arsenic poisoning.

Question 81 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A person was brought by police from the railway platform. He is talking irrelevant. He has a dry mouth with hot skin, dilated pupil, staggering gait and slurred speech the most probable diagnosis:

Options:
  Alkohol intoxication
  Carbamate poisoning
  Organophosphorus poisoning
✓ Dhatura poisoning
  Capsicum poisoning

Explanation:
The signs (dry mouth, hot skin, dilated pupils, confusion) represent the anticholinergic toxidrome, characteristic of Dhatura (Atropine/Hyoscine) poisoning.

  Lead
✓ Arsenic
  Thallium
  Mercury
  Copper

Mees' lines and raindrop pigmentation are key features of Chronic Arsenic Poisoning.

Question 83 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A house wife ingests a rodenticide white powder accidentally. She is brought to hospital where the examination shows generalized, flaccid paralysis and an irregular pulse Electrocardiogram shows multiple ventricular ectopics generalized changes with ST-T. Serum potassium is 2.5mEq/L. The most likely ingested poison is:

Options:
  Arsenic
  Lead
  Mercury
  Strychnine
✓ Thallium

Explanation:
Thallium poisoning (often found in rodenticides) causes severe peripheral neuropathy (flaccid paralysis), cardiac instability, and profound hypokalemia.

Question 84 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A middle aged woman swallowed 50 ml of solution used for photographic purposes. The quantity was 300 mg in 2 minutes she became unconscious. The whole of the body was slightly convulsed, the pupils were dilated she had frothing the pulse was weak and she died within 20 minutes. The most possible solution is?

Options:
  Phosphorus
  Carbolic acid
✓ Potassium cyanide
  Arsenic
  Mercury

Explanation:
Extremely rapid onset of symptoms (unconsciousness in 2 minutes, death in 20 minutes) is characteristic of cyanide poisoning. Cyanide salts were historically used in photography fixatives.

================================================================================
Question 85 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
After skin contamination the patient passed into coma with miosis and finally acute nephritis. The poison responsible is:

Options:
  Oxalic acid
  Nitric acid
✓ Carbolic acid
  Sulphuric acid
  Hydrocyanic acid

Explanation:
Carbolic acid (Phenol) is highly lipophilic, rapidly absorbed through the skin, and causes systemic toxicity including CNS depression (coma), miosis, and renal damage (acute nephritis).

================================================================================
Question 86 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
In case of food borne botulism the toxin is:

Options:
✓ Formed in the canned food before consumption
  Formed in the mouth
  Formed in the duodenum
  Formed in the colon
  Formed in the stomach

Explanation:
Foodborne botulism is an intoxication; the toxin is produced by C. botulinum spores growing anaerobically, typically in improperly preserved food (like canned goods), and is ingested pre-formed.

Question 88 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The following should be avoided in neutralization of acid present in the stomach in acid poisoning:

Options:
  Water
  Milk
✓ Weak Alkali
  Milk with magnesium oxide
  All of above

Explanation:
Alkaline solutions should be avoided in acid poisoning (and vice versa) due to the risk of severe exothermic reaction leading to thermal injury and perforation.

Castor oil is a following kind of poison:

Options:
✓ Irritant
  Deliriant
  Inebriant
  Narcotic
  Corrosive

Explanation:
Castor oil contains ricin, a highly potent cytotoxin (irritant poison).

Question 90 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The fatal dose of croton seeds is:

Options:
  One
✓ Four
  Ten
  Fifty
  Hundred

Explanation:
The fatal dose of Croton seeds is generally reported as 3 to 4 seeds due to the presence of crotin, a highly irritant toxic protein.

Question 91 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
The active principle in ratti seeds is:

Options:
  Crotin
  Ricin
  Cannabinol
✓ Abrin
  Paracetamol

The ratti seed (Abrus precatorius) contains the highly toxic protein Abrin.

Question 92 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A community health worker is conducting a survey to determine the prevalence of anemia in a village. She randomly selects 200 women of childbearing age and tests their hemoglobin levels. She finds that 40 women have anemia. What is the prevalence of anemia in the village?

Options:
  10%
✓ 20%
  30%
  40%
  50%

Explanation:
Prevalence is calculated as (Number of existing cases / Total population at that time) * 100. (40 / 200) * 100 = 20%.

================================================================================
Question 93 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
In a city of 500,000 people with no old cases of TB, 2000 people were diagnosed with tuberculosis in a year. What is the incidence rate/1000 population during that year?

Options:
  0.4 per 1000
✓ 4 per 1000
  40 per 1000
  400 per 1000
  4000 per 1000

Explanation:
Incidence Rate = (New cases / Population at risk) * multiplier. (2000 / 500,000) * 1000 = 4 per 1000 population.

================================================================================
Question 94 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
In which of the following circumstances the prevalence of a disease in a population will increase all else being constant?

Options:
  If population in which disease is measured increases
  If recovery of the disease is faster
  If secondary attack rate falls
✓ If survival time of disease increases
  If the incidence rate falls

Explanation:
Prevalence ≈ Incidence × Duration. If the survival time (duration) of a chronic disease increases (e.g., due to improved treatment), prevalence increases.

================================================================================
Question 95 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
In an epidemiological context, what is the population is at risk?

Options:
  A group of people participating in a study that may be harmful to them
✓ Group of people who are not diseased but susceptible to disease
  Population group with highest attributable risk of disease
  Population group with highest relative risk of disease
  The proportion of population that engages in risky behavior

Explanation:
The population at risk (denominator for incidence) includes individuals who are currently disease-free but susceptible to developing the disease.

================================================================================
Question 96 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Chernobyl tragedy is an example of:

Options:
  Continuous epidemic
  Modern epidemic
✓ Point source epidemic
  Propagated epidemic
  Slow epidemic

Explanation:
A point source epidemic occurs when people are exposed to the same source over a relatively brief period. The Chernobyl explosion provided a singular, albeit massive, instantaneous environmental exposure.

================================================================================
Question 97 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A village water supply is contaminated with industrial waste for a long time, resulting in increased number of chemical poisoning cases for past many years. What type of epidemic is this?

Options:
✓ Continous epidemic
  Modern epidemic
  Point source epidemic
  Propagated epidemic
  Slow epidemic

Explanation:
A continuous epidemic results from prolonged and ongoing exposure to the same source over an extended period (chronic contamination of water supply).

================================================================================
Question 98 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which indicator shows the risk of developing a disease?

Options:
  Case fatality rate
✓ Incidence rate
  Period prevalence
  Point prevalence
  Recovery rate

Explanation:
Incidence rate measures the rate at which new cases of a disease occur over a specified time period in a population at risk, thereby reflecting the risk of developing the disease.

================================================================================
Question 99 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Population at risk is used as a denominator for calculation of a rate. Which of the following rates uses population at risk as denominator?

Options:
  Generation time
✓ Incidence rate
  Point prevalence
  Proportional mortality ratio
  Standardized mortality ratio

Explanation:
The denominator for the Incidence Rate is the population at risk (the population that can potentially develop the disease).

================================================================================
Question 100 (GMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An expert in the field of public health is required to estimate the magnitude of iodine deficiency disorders. He will use this information for planning purposes. Which rate would he calculate for this?

Options:
  Case fatality
  Incidence
✓ Prevalence
  Proportionate mortality
  Total mortality

Explanation:
Prevalence measures the proportion of a population currently affected by a disease, which best describes the magnitude (burden) of a chronic public health problem.

Question 101 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
A new disease of respiratory system affected a large proportion of population over a wide geographic area including many continents in 2019. What this kind of disease spread is called?

Options:
  Epidemic
  Sporadic
✓ Pandemic
  Endemic
  Opportunistic

Explanation:
A Pandemic refers to an epidemic that has spread over several countries or continents, usually affecting a large number of people (e.g., COVID-19).

  BHU

  Teaching hospital
✓ THQ

Question 104 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
In a poor community, there in high prevalence of acute diarrhea cases. The best method for preventing this health Problem in the long run is:

Options:
  Anti-diarrheal drugs
  Immunization against cholera and typhoid
  Living in mosquito proof zone
✓ Provision of sanitary latrine
  Use of fresh vegetables

Explanation:
Diarrheal diseases are prevented long-term by interrupting the fecal-oral route, primarily through sanitation (sanitary latrines) and clean water supply.

  Transmits by oral route

  Case fatality
  Cause specific mortality
✓ Incidence
  Prevalence
  Proportionate mortality

✓ Appropriate technology

  Suitability

Question 111 (GMC - 2023)

Question:
All of the following involved in the life cycle of Echinococcus granulosus except:

Options:
  Dog
  Sheep
  Camel
  Man
✓ Cat

Echinococcus granulosus uses dogs (definitive host) and sheep/camel/man (intermediate hosts). Cats are not typically involved.

Question 112 (GMC - 2023)

A female Ascaris can be identified on which basis?

  Curved posterior end
  Common cloacal aperture
  Presence of post anal papillar
✓ Straight posterior end
  Two spicules found at the posterior end

Explanation:
Female Ascaris worms have a straight posterior end, whereas male worms have a curved (hooked) posterior end with spicules.

✓ Amebiasis
  Histoplasmosis
  Mycobacterial infection
  Salmonella typhi

  Cystic fibrosis

  Tropical sprue
  Lactose intolerance

  KRAS

  P16

Question 116 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:

Options:
  Esophageal varices
  Peptic ulcer
  Gastritis
✓ Mucosal tears at gastroesophageal junction
  Iatrogenic perforation

  Colon
  Small intestine
  Esophagus
✓ Stomach
  Appendix

Explanation:
Linitis plastica ('leather bottle stomach') is a morphological term for diffuse infiltrating gastric adenocarcinoma where the stomach wall becomes thickened and rigid.

Question 120 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
50 year old man presenting with nausea for 5 years, physical exam no abnormal finding. He undergoes upper GI endoscopy and a small area of gastric fundal mucosa has loss of rugal folds. Biopsies taken and microscopic reveal well differentiated adenocarcinoma. Mucosa of upper GI five years back showed pattern of chronic inflammation (gastritis). Which one was the most likely risk factor?

Options:
  Inherent APC mutation
✓ H Pylori infection
  Chronic alcohol
  Chronic use of nsaids

Explanation:
Chronic H. pylori infection is the strongest risk factor for gastric adenocarcinoma, causing chronic gastritis and subsequent atrophy (loss of rugal folds) and neoplasia.

Question 121 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 39 year old man is having a routine physical examination because of history of colon cancer in his family he has no abdominal tenderness or masses and active bowel sounds are present. However his stool is positive for occult blood. Colonoscopy is performed. there are 7 polyps found in ascending colon. 3 of these are small 0.5cm pedunculated tubular adenomas. 3 are 1 cm tubulovillous adenomas and 1 is 2cm sessile villous adenoma. In the cecum Containing a focus of well differentiated adenocarcinoma. Which of the following is most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  PTEN associated syndrome
  Chronic ulcerative colitis
✓ HNPCC
  Adenomatous polyposis coli
  Peutz jehgers syndrome

Explanation:
Hereditary Non-Polyposis Colorectal Cancer (HNPCC/Lynch Syndrome) typically presents with few adenomas (not hundreds like FAP), right-sided (cecal) tumors, and a strong family history.

Question 139 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
PPI use as first choice although it Hy short half life and one dose daily because of ..

Options:
  Zero order kinetics
✓ Irreversibly block receptors
  Prodrug
  High efficacy
  Slow excretion

Explanation:
PPIs irreversibly inhibit the H+/K+ ATPase proton pump. New pumps must be synthesized to restore acid secretion, leading to a prolonged duration of action despite a short plasma half-life.

  Gastric and acetylcholine secretion increases after dinner

Question 165 (KGMC - 2023)

Hypocalcemia is the symptom of poison:

Options:
  Carbolic acid
✓ Oxalic acid
  Nitric acid
  Sulphuric acid
  Hydrocyanide

Question 167 (KGMC - 2023)

  H2SO4
✓ HNO3
  None

✓ Hydrofloric acid

  Sulphuric acid

Question 191 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
The following factors associated with virulence of h.pylori chronic infection except:

Options:
✓ Mucous
  Flagella
  Urease
  Toxins
  Adhesions

Explanation:
Mucus is a protective layer of the stomach, not a virulence factor used by H. pylori. The bacteria's virulence relies on motility (flagella), acid neutralization (urease), cell damage (toxins), and binding (adhesions).

Question 200 (KMC - 2023)

Question:

Options:
  Diarrhea is always presents
  Gross rectal bleeding is always presents
  Inflammation is confined to mucosa except in severe cases
  Inflammation is uniform and diffuse
✓ Rectum is often spared

Explanation:
Rectal sparing is a characteristic feature of Crohn's disease, whereas the rectum is always involved in Ulcerative Colitis.

  Gastric lavage only

  Gastric lavage with calcium carbonate
  Gastric lavage with diluents
  Gastric lavage with universal antidote

  Anti hepatitis A IgG

Explanation:
The clinical picture of acute hepatitis in a child with a contact history suggests Hepatitis A. IgM anti-HAV is the confirmatory test.

================================================================================
Question 239 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4 days old baby girl born to G3P2 mother, presented with jaundice since her first day of life. Mother's blood group is O-ive and baby blood group is A+ive. On examination, baby is pale and icteric. Her total S bilirubin is 27 mg /dl , indirect bilirubin is 25.5mg/dl. The most likely cause of jaundice in this baby is:

Options:
✓ ABO blood group incompatibility
  Both ABO and Rh incompatibility
  G6PD deficiency
  Physiological jaundice
  Rh incompatibility

Explanation:
Jaundice on the first day of life is pathological. With Mother O-ve and Baby A+ve, both ABO and Rh incompatibility are possible. However, ABO incompatibility is more common, though Rh is more severe. Given the specific setup (O mother, A baby), ABO incompatibility is a classic cause. Rh incompatibility requires sensitization. But ABO is 'most likely' in general terms if Rh status isn't specified as sensitized, but here Rh incompatibility (O-ve mother, +ve baby) is very high risk if not first pregnancy. Wait, G3P2. If Rhogam not given, Rh is very likely. However, ABO is also present. Usually, ABO causes early jaundice. But Rh causes *severe* early jaundice. Options separate them. A+ baby has A antigen (ABO mismatch) and D antigen (Rh mismatch). Both exist. Option (b) says 'Both'. This is technically true. But often single best answer is needed. Rh is more likely to cause severe hyperbilirubinemia (27mg/dl). But if I must choose, 'Both' covers the full immunological risk.

Question 240 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 3 year old child presented to OPD with history of loose stools and vomiting for the past two days. On examination his temperature is 99F. He is lethargic, unable to drink and his skin pinch goes back very slowly. How will you manage this patient?

Options:
  Plan A for rehydration, zinc, oral antibiotics
  Plan B for rehydration, zinc, oral antibiotics
  Plan B for rehydration and zinc
  Plan C for rehydration, zinc, oral antibiotics
✓ Plan C for rehydration and zinc

Explanation:
The signs (lethargic, unable to drink, very slow skin pinch) indicate Severe Dehydration, requiring Plan C (IV fluids). Antibiotics are not indicated unless there is dysentery or cholera, which isn't specified.

Question:
A 58 years old woman is brought to the emergency with sudden onset pain in the abdomen for the last two hours. The pain is agonizing in nature and she has a history of NSAIDS use for her arthritis. Peptic ulcer perforation is suspected, what investigation would be diagnosed in this situation?

  Serum amylase levels
  Urea breath test
  Ultrasound of the abdomen
  Upper GI endoscopy
✓ X ray chest PA view

Erect Chest X-ray (PA view) is the initial diagnostic test for perforated peptic ulcer to look for free gas under the diaphragm.

Question:

Question 244 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 70 year old man presented with severe abdominal pain that radiates to the back. All of the following investigations will help in diagnosis except?

Options:
  Abdominal X ray
  Abdominal ultrasound
✓ Serum alpha feto protein
  Serum amylase
  Total leukocytes count

Explanation:
Serum Alpha-Fetoprotein (AFP) is a tumor marker for hepatocellular carcinoma and germ cell tumors, not useful for diagnosing acute abdominal pain/pancreatitis.

Question 245 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55 years old male presents with features of obstructive jaundice. He also report weight loss of 7 kgs in the last 2 months. On CT scan, the CBD is dilated till the lower end and the main pancreatic duct is also dilated. Pancreas is normal. What is most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Choledocholithiasis
  Carcinoma gall bladder
  Hilar cholangiocarcinoma
✓ Periampullary carcinoma
  Hepatoma

Explanation:
The 'double duct sign' (dilatation of both CBD and pancreatic duct) combined with obstructive jaundice and significant weight loss, in the absence of a visible pancreatic mass, strongly suggests Periampullary Carcinoma (e.g., distal CBD or ampulla of Vater tumor).

✓ Hemorrhoids
  Rectal prolapse
  Solitary rectal ulcer
  Carcinoma rectum

Explanation:
Painless, bright red bleeding separate from stool (usually described as coating the stool or dripping) and associated itching is the classic presentation of internal Hemorrhoids.

Question 247 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 old man with established cirrhosis had a surveillance ultrasound scan. It showed a 2 cms focal Liver lesion. AFP levels were normal. What is the most appropriate next step?

Options:
✓ Contrast enhanced CT scan
  Liver transplant referral
  Repeat AFP at 6 weeks
  Repeat USS at 6 weeks
  Ultrasound guided liver biopsy

Explanation:
In a cirrhotic patient, a suspicious focal liver lesion requires multi-phasic imaging (CT or MRI with contrast) for definitive characterization (wash-in/wash-out pattern) to rule out hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC).

Question 248 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60 year old man with established cirrhosis underwent a six monthly surveillance ultrasound scan (USS). It showed a suspicious focal Liver lesion. A subsequent contrast CT scan of abdomen confirmed a 2.5 CMS focal lesion in the liver. Serum alpha Fetoproteins were raised at 200 ng/ml. A previous AFP and USS were normal 6 months earlier. What is the most appropriate next step?

Options:
✓ Liver transplant referral
  PET scan
  Repeat scan at 6 weeks
  Repeat AFP in y months
  Ultrasound guided liver biopsy

Explanation:
In a cirrhotic patient, a lesion >2 cm combined with an AFP >200 ng/mL is often sufficient for a non-invasive diagnosis of hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC). The next step is usually staging and treatment planning, for which referral to a specialized center (e.g., for liver transplant assessment) is necessary.

Question 251 (KMC - 2023)

✓ Refer to gastroenterologist for colonoscopy
  Excise the ulcer and biopsy

Question 255 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A dead was presented to a department of Forensic Medicine, KMC, Peshawar for postmortem examination, where in autopsy report revealed blue line gums, ulceration of GIT wall , on X-ray lead line was recorded, and bone marrow hyperplasia was also noted. What is fatal period in poisoning of all such cases?

Options:
  12-24hours
  1-2 days
  2-3 days
  3-4 days
✓ 4-5 days

Explanation:
The symptoms describe acute exacerbation of chronic lead poisoning. While chronic poisoning lasts months/years, the final fatal period in severe acute/subacute heavy metal poisoning is often days, with 4-5 days being a plausible timeframe for irreversible organ failure.

Question 256 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A dead body to autopsy room KMC, Peshawar. Upon examination it had bitter almond like smell, cherry red color postmortem lividity, cyanosed face, thighly clenched jaw, and hemorrhages on the serosal surfaces. In such cases, spleen is considered to be best sample for chemical analysis. What would be the possible findings by the chemical analyzer in such poisoning case?

Options:
  Decrease number of RBCs
  Degenerated RBCs
  Increase number of RBCs
✓ Normal RBCs
  Ruptured RBCs

Explanation:
Cyanide poisoning causes cherry red discoloration because it prevents cellular oxygen utilization (cytotoxic anoxia), meaning oxygen remains bound to hemoglobin. The RBCs themselves remain structurally and numerically normal.

Question 257 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
You are posted as casualty medical officer in a district Headquarter hospital. A patient was received with headache as the only symptom. He brought some sample of a poison which was identified to be potassium cyanide. Cyanide is ineffective in which of the following conditions?

Options:
  Have low acid in stomach
  Have hyperacidity
  Low dose
✓ No acid in stomach (achlorhydria)
  Obese persons

Explanation:
Cyanide salts (e.g., KCN) require gastric acid (HCl) to be converted into the volatile and rapidly absorbed toxic gas Hydrogen Cyanide (HCN). In achlorhydria, this conversion is prevented, rendering the salt ineffective.

Options:
✓ Blood
  Liver
  Lungs
  Spleen
  Urine

Explanation:
Burning plastic produces cyanide and carbon monoxide (both cause cytotoxic anoxia/asphyxia). Both poisons are confirmed primarily by analyzing the concentration in Blood.

Question 260 (KMC - 2023)

✓ Oxalic acid
  Zinc sulphate

Question:

  Alt
  Conjugated bilirubin
  Galactosyltransaminase

Explanation:
Physiological jaundice of the newborn (especially in preterm infants) is characterized by an elevation of Unconjugated Bilirubin due to immature hepatic enzymes (UGT).

Question:
A 60 kg oldman, presented to opd with a hx of taking 2 packs cigarettes, 5 cups of coffee and 6 cups of beer, on examination he has tender abdomen wd a palpable liver feeling 2cm below lower margin of costal cartilage, on biopsy he has macrovescicular steatohepatitis, if he restrain himself, what would be the result?

  Progress to cirrhosis
  Progress to acute hepatitis
  To HCC
  Remain unchanged
✓ Revert to normal

Explanation:
Alcoholic steatosis and early steatohepatitis are potentially reversible ('Revert to normal') with abstinence from alcohol.

  Subacute fulminant failure

✓ Hepatitis B

Question:

  Hepatitis D IgM antibody
  Hepatitis C antibody
✓ Hepatitis A IgM antibody
  Hepatitis B core antibody

Question 298 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:

✓ Hemorrhoids
  Perianal hematoma
  Rectal polyp
  Rectal prolapsed

✓ Adhesive bowel obstruction/diseases
  Crohn's disease
  Intussusception
  Intra-abdominal abscess
  Sigmoid Volvulus

Explanation:
Adhesions from prior surgery (hemicolectomy) are the most common cause of small bowel obstruction. The symptoms (colicky pain, vomiting, distension, tinkling bowel sounds) indicate obstruction.

Question 300 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which one of these enzyme appear earlier and persist longer in intra and extra hepatic obstruction?

Options:
✓ ALP (alkaline phosphatase)
  ALT
  AST
  GGT (gamma glutamyl transferase)
  5'N (5' nucleotidase)

Explanation:
Alkaline Phosphatase (ALP) rises earlier and persists longer than bilirubin in biliary obstruction. While GGT and 5'N are also markers, ALP is the standard primary marker for cholestasis.

Question 301 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
From the last 6 days, a previously healthy 38-year-old woman has become increasingly obtunded. She has scleral yellow discoloration. She is afebrile and has a blood pressure of 110/55 mm Hg. Laboratory investigations reveal a prothrombin time of 38 seconds (with a control of 13), an ALT level of 1854 U/L, AST level of 1621 U/L, and serum albumin concentration of 1.8 g/dL. Which of the following additional serum laboratory test findings would you most likely expect to be present?

Options:
  Hepatitis C virus antibody
  Increased alkaline phosphatase level
  Increased amylase level
✓ Increased ammonia level
  Positive antinuclear antibody

Explanation:
The patient has acute fulminant liver failure (encephalopathy/obtundation, coagulopathy, massive transaminitis). Elevated serum ammonia is a key finding in hepatic encephalopathy.

Question 302 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old female has experienced gradually increasing malaise, Jaundice, and loss of appetite for the last 5 months. She has a total bilirubin concentration of 6.8 mg/dL, AST of 189 U/L, ALT of 200 U/L, and alkaline phosphatase of 36 U/L. A liver biopsy shows piecemeal necrosis of hepatocytes at the limiting plate with portal fibrosis and a mononuclear infiltrate in the portal tracts. These findings are most typical for?

Options:
  Congestive heart failure
  Choledocholithiasis
  HAV infection
  Hemochromatosis
✓ HCV infection

Explanation:
Chronic Hepatitis C is characterized by chronic inflammation (mononuclear infiltrate), interface hepatitis (piecemeal necrosis), and fibrosis. The duration (5 months) and biopsy findings fit chronic viral hepatitis.

Question:

Options:
✓ Fundic glands polyps
  Gastric adenomas
  Hyperplastic polyps
  Hypertrophic gastropathy
  Inflammatory polyps

Fundic gland polyps are associated with long-term PPI use. Histologically, they show cystically dilated glands lined by parietal and chief cells in the gastric body/fundus.

Question:

✓ Ischemic colitis
  Ulcerative colitis
  Bacterial Dysentery
  Irritable bowel syndrome

Question 307 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 47-year-old woman presents to your clinic with a three-month history of dysphagia. There is no history of drastic weight loss and the patient experiences symptoms when swallowing solids but not liquids. Which of the following is not an obstructive cause of dysphagia?

Options:
  Pharyngeal carcinoma
  Esophageal web
  Retrosternal goiter
  Peptic stricture
✓ Myasthenia Gravis

Explanation:
Myasthenia Gravis causes neuromuscular (propulsive) dysphagia, not obstructive (mechanical) dysphagia. Solids-only dysphagia usually points to mechanical obstruction.

  There is significant risk for development of hepatocellular carcinoma.

The serology (HBsAg +, IgM anti-HBc +) confirms Acute Hepatitis B infection. In immunocompetent adults, complete recovery occurs in >95% of cases.

Question 311 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 48-year-old man has increasing abdominal girth and icterus. Serum laboratory findings include a total bilirubin concentration of 5.2 mg/dL, direct bilirubin of 4.2 mg/dL, alkaline phosphatase of 95 U/L, aspartate aminotransferase (AST) of 300 U/L, alanine aminotransferase (ALT) of 158 U/L, total protein concentration of 6.4 g/dl, and albumin concentration of 2.2 g/dL. The prothrombin time is 18 seconds (control, 12 seconds). The blood ammonia level is 105 micromol/L. The most likely cause for these findings is?

Options:
  Acute hepatitis A infection
✓ Alcoholic liver disease
  Choledocholithiasis
  Metastatic adenocarcinoma
  Primary biliary cirrhosis

Explanation:
The AST:ALT ratio > 2:1 (300:158) is characteristic of Alcoholic Liver Disease. The hypoalbuminemia and coagulopathy indicate chronic/severe liver dysfunction (cirrhosis).

  Odynophagia
✓ Halitosis
  Recurrent vomiting
  Occult or gross gastrointestinal bleeding
  Anemia

Question 325 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following is the most important predictor of whether a patient with viral hepatitis will develop chronic liver disease with progression to cirrhosis?

Options:
  The presence of chronic inflammatory cells in the portal tract
  The degree to which hepatic transaminase enzymes are elevated
  The length of time that hepatic enzymes remain elevated
✓ The specific form of hepatitis virus responsible for the infection
  The presence of inflammatory cells in the sinusoids on a liver biopsy

Explanation:
The viral etiology is the strongest predictor. Hepatitis C has a high rate of chronicity (80%), Hepatitis B varies by age (low in adults), and Hepatitis A never causes chronic disease.

  Wilson disease

Question 327 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 59 year old woman suffering from chronic constipation routinely self administered milk of magnesia (magnesium hydroxide) daily. Which of the following actions most likely mediated the laxative effect of the drug?

Options:
  Formation of bulkey emollient gel
  Inhibition of cholecystokinin release
  Lubrication of fecal material
✓ Retention of water in vessels by osmosis
  Stretching of intestinal wall

Explanation:
Magnesium hydroxide is an osmotic laxative. Poorly absorbed magnesium ions retain water in the intestinal lumen via osmosis, softening the stool.

Options:
  Bisacodyl
  Castor oil
✓ Docusate
  Magnesium hydroxide
  Senna

Explanation:
Docusate (stool softener) or bulk-forming agents are generally considered safe first-line options for constipation in pregnancy. Stimulants (Castor oil) are contraindicated.

Question 334 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 6-month-old infant presents with diarrhea and vomiting for the past 24 hours. The infant appears lethargic, has sunken eyes, and decreased urine output. What is the most appropriate next step?

  Prescribe oral rehydration solution (ORS) and advise a follow-up appointment

  Recommend over-the-counter anti-diarrheal medication
  Order stool culture and initiate empiric antibiotic treatment
  Provide home care instructions and reassurance

Explanation:
The infant has signs of severe dehydration (lethargy, sunken eyes). Immediate IV fluid resuscitation is required.

Question 335 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 46-year-old man recently diagnosed with a duodenal ulcer started a treatment that included daily Sucralfate. Which of the following mechanisms most likely mediates the therapeutic efficacy of the drug in the patient's disease?

Options:
✓ Acting as a barrier to acid by binding to necrotic ulcer tissue
  Exerting a bactericidal effect against Helicobacter pylori
  Inhibiting hydrochloric acid secretion
  Reacting with gastric hydrochloric acid to form salt and water
  Stimulating bicarbonate secretion by antral parietal cells

Explanation:
Sucralfate polymerizes in the acidic environment to form a sticky gel that adheres to the ulcer crater (necrotic tissue), creating a protective barrier.

Options:
✓ Antacids
  Belladonna alkaloids
  H2 blockers
  Misoprostol
  Propantheline

Explanation:
Antacids neutralize acid rapidly, providing immediate (fastest) but short-term relief.

Question 338 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient has severe gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD). In addition to providing some immediate symptom relief, for which we will prescribe usually effective doses of an OTC combination antacid product, we want to suppress gastric acid as fully as possible. Which of the following drug is most likely to meet that criterion?

Options:
  Atropine
  Calcium carbonate
  Cimetidine
✓ Esomeprazole
  Misoprostol

Explanation:
Proton Pump Inhibitors like Esomeprazole provide the most potent and complete suppression of gastric acid secretion compared to H2 blockers or antacids.

Question 339 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A physician is treating a patient with dysentery caused by Shigella infection. The patient's travel history indicates recent travel to a developing country. What is the most appropriate treatment for this patient?

Options:
✓ Prescribing antibiotics based on local antibiotic resistance patterns
  Administering a broad-spectrum antiviral medication
  Advising bed rest and increased fluid intake only
  Recommending a vaccine for long-term protection
  Performing a surgical procedure to remove the infected intestine

Explanation:
Shigellosis (dysentery) is treated with antibiotics (like fluoroquinolones or azithromycin) to shorten the course and reduce transmission, chosen based on resistance patterns.

Question 340 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
We have two patients. One requires suppression of emesis caused by an anticancer drug that causes a high incidence and severity of vomiting (a highly emetogenic drug). Another patient has severe diabetic gastroparesis and gastroesophageal reflux, which requires relief. Which drug would be most suitable for both indications (assuming no specific contraindications)?

Options:
  Diphenoxylate
  Dronabinol
  Loperamide
✓ Metoclopramide
  Ondansetron

Explanation:
Metoclopramide has both antiemetic properties (central D2 blockade) and prokinetic properties (treats gastroparesis/GERD).

Question 341 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45-year-old woman presented to her physician because of a 2-week history of painless diarrhea usually occurred during meals. Options:
  5-HT3 serotonergic
  Alpha-2 adrenergic
  M3 cholinergic
✓ Mu opioid
  Nn cholinergic

Explanation:
Loperamide is an agonist at mu-opioid receptors in the enteric nervous system, reducing peristalsis.

Question:

Options:
  Adrenergic agonists
  Dopaminergic agonist
  GABAergic agonists
  Muscarinic antagonists
✓ Serotonergic antagonists

Serotonin (5-HT3) antagonists (like Ondansetron) are the standard of care for prophylaxis of chemotherapy-induced nausea and vomiting.

Question 343 (NWSM - 2023)

Question:
A 45-year-old woman had been self-medicating for heartburn. The preparation she was using was a combination of magnesium hydroxide and aluminum hydroxide. Which of the following reasons best explains why many antacid preparations on the market contain a combination of these two antacids instead of a single product?

Options:
  To achieve a bactericidal effect on Helicobacter pylori
  To avoid antacid overdose toxicity
  To avoid interaction with other drugs given concomitantly
✓ To decrease the occurrence of constipation or diarrhea
  To facilitate gastric emptying

Explanation:
Magnesium salts cause diarrhea, while Aluminum salts cause constipation. Combining them counteracts these opposing side effects.

Question 344 (NWSM - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
During your on call, you are bleeped to see an 80 year old women on the ward who has not opened her bowels for the last 4 days. She is not known to have a history of constipation. On examination her observations are within normal range, the abdomen is soft and there is mild discomfort at the left iliac fossa. Bowel sounds are present and on PR examination the rectum is empty. You consult your registrar who asks you to prescribe osmotic laxative. What is the most appropriate treatment.

Options:
  Ispaghula Husk
  Docusate sodium
✓ Lactulose
  Senna
  Methylcellulose

Explanation:
Lactulose is the osmotic laxative listed. Ispaghula/Methylcellulose are bulk-forming, Docusate is a softener, Senna is a stimulant.
